Genealogical reference document "Descendants of MacNaughton Family" (Family Series 4) spanning 11 generations, part of Don's comprehensive family documentation. MacNaughton family appears to be significant in Don's family history, likely through maternal connections. The 11-generation scope suggests deep genealogical research spanning from early Scotland through multiple New Brunswick family lines. Continues Don's systematic approach to documenting all family branches contributing to his identity and heritage.


Descendants of MacNaughton Family



Generation No. 1


1.  MACNAUGHTON1 FAMILY


Notes for MACNAUGHTON FAMILY:



The first in line is John MacNauchtan, thought to have been born at Dunderave Castle, Scotland and died (before 1750) at Glenlyon, Perthshire, Scotland.  His son, Malcolm was also thought to have been born at Dunderave Castle and died at Glenlyon, Perthshire, Scotland. Following the publication of this information in Family Chronicle No. 28, however, I received the following Email from  Thomas Holme:


 "There were no McNaughtons living in Dunderave castle after around 1720. Your website states that Malcolm's son John was born in Dunderave castle July 24, 1774. But the MacNaughtons lost the castle and all their lands in the first decade of the 1700s. By 1711 they final paperwork was finished and Dunderave no longer belonged to them. Dunderave belonged to the family of Campbel of Ardkinglas that lived across Loch Fyne from the MacNauchtans. We are sure that a few members of the MacNauchtan family continued to dwell in the castle for perhaps another ten years in hope that some way would develop to return the lands and castle to the MacNauchtans. But that did not happen. Famous historians living in that time write that the castle was abandoned and none of the MacNauchtan family lived there afterwards. So you see, he couldn't have been born in Dunderave in 1774".


Malcolm's son, John, was  born July 24, 1774; he married Catherine Stewart and they had eight children.  John died in Scotland.  


Alexander (1789-1819) married Janet Jack (1782-1872)

Christina (1785-1865) married Robert Weeds (1788-1872)

Duncan (1787-1824) married Margaret MacPherson (

William (

Donald (1795-1820) married Margaret MacBeath (        -1857)

Mary (1799-1829) married John Cameron (1795-1879)

Malcolm (1797-1865) married Barbara MacBeath (1806-1875)

John (1801-         ) married Marjorie Cameron (1807-1878)


Notes from Donald Edge, whose mother was a MacNaughton and a direct descendant of the early MacNaughtons, reveal that, according to the 1851 census, Christina MacNaughton came to New Brunswick in 1812; she was joined by three brothers, Alexander, Donald and Malcolm in October, 1817 and Mary in November of the same year.  In 1819, the family was joined by their mother, Catherine and two other brothers, Duncan and John.  They were granted land on the north side of the Black River, Alexander being granted 296 acres and John, Donald and Malcolm 200 acres each.  Duncan was killed by a falling tree in 1824 and was the second person buried in the Black River Cemetery 







Child of MACNAUGHTON FAMILY is:

2. i. JOHN2 MACNAUCHTAN, b. Glen Lyon, Perthshire, Scotland; d. Glen Lyon, Perthshire, Scotland.

Did not come to Canada; buried in Scotland; Donald Edge shows his death before 1819.

Generation No. 5


5.  ALEXANDER5 MACNAUGHTON (JOHN4, MALCOLM3 MACNAUGHTAN, JOHN2 MACNAUCHTAN, MACNAUGHTON1 FAMILY)1 was born 1789 in Perthshire, Scotland, and died April 28, 1849.  He married JANET JACK1 Abt. 1819.  She was born Abt. 1782, and died 1872.


Notes for ALEXANDER MACNAUGHTON:

On Dec. 1, 2009, Helen Proudfoot wrote  "I've found Mary's birth in Dollar - in 1818 as you surmised - and have downloaded the Kirk records for her, Catherine and Christian.  None of the 3 give much information, except to confirm that he [Alexander McNaughton] was a coal grieve throughout the period, which I imagine would be better paid than mere labouring,  though still long hours and extremely unpleasant 


I also looked up the marriage records.  Not unusually they [Alexander McNaughton & Janet Jack] were married twice - once in Edinburgh and then again in Tillicoultry a few days later. Tthe Edinburgh kirk records show little about Alexander except that he was a shepherd at the time.  It is more informative about Janet.  Her father was called John Jac and was dead by 1817.  The witnesses were John Jack, a teacher in Leith and Robert Jack a quarrier in Abby (sic) Hill.  Both seem to have made their mark rather than signed.  The Tillicoultry register gives no useful information I'm afraid.


I did have a look to see whether I could find an obvious John Jack, with children Janet, John and Robert (assuming John and Robert were Janet's brothers rather than one or both of them an uncle - maybe not a safe assumption, but for the purposes of searching the only reasonable one I could make and still tie down any possible positive hits).


There seem to have been at least 5 women in the period 1760-1810 in St Cuthbert's who were married to men called John Jack.  Of course child birth was such a killer at the time that some of these women may have been married to the same John Jack.  Unfortunately none of them that I can trace had a Janet, a John and  a Robert.  Of course if the family came from outside St Cuthbert's, we'd be looking for a needle in a haystack.  In terms of deaths, the best I could come up with was a 60 year old John Jack, a shoemaker, who was buried at St Cuthbert's in 1808.  The date and age would fit well enough, but it's really only circumstantial evidence."




More About ALEXANDER MACNAUGHTON:

Emigration: 1817


Notes for JANET JACK:

Believed to have come to Canada in October 1817, although some think 1824;1851 Census shows her living with her daughter, Christina and Jubal and gives her age as 60.  Chatham Union Advocate of April 10, 1872 reports "Died, at the residence of her son-in-law 21 inst, Janet Jack M'Naughton relict of Alexander MacNaughton, age 90."

Notes for MARY MACNAUGHTON:

Thanks to Barry MacKenzie for the following: Date : November 6, 1871, County : York, Place : Fredericton, Newspaper : Colonial Farmer, "d. Black River (North. Co.) 9th ult., of consumption, Mary McNaughton WATTLING w/o William WATTLING, age 47, left husband, nine children."



More About MARY MACNAUGHTON:

Burial: Black River, North'ld Co, New Brunswick


Notes for WILLIAM WATLING:

William came to Canada in 1830, although the 1851 census says 1836, and, according to the census of 1851, was living in Glenelg (Black River) with his wife Mary MacNaughton, and daughters, Susan and Janet; also in the household was a 24 year old step-brother, Henry Watling. thanks to Barry MacKenzie for the following:  "Date : October 11, 1895, County : Saint John Place : Saint John, Newspaper : The Daily Sun, Chatham (North. Co.) Oct. 10 - For 65 years William WATLING was a resident of Miramichi. He came to this country from England with his parents in 1830 and was 75 years of age at the time of his death. He was laid away in the Black River burial ground. Four years ago he removed from Black River to Black Brook where he resided up to the time of his death with W.S. BABKIRK." (Ed.  Babkirk was a son-in-law)



More About WILLIAM WATLING:

Baptism: July 11, 1814

Burial: Black River, North'ld Co, New Brunswick

Notes for JUBAL WATLING:

The 1851 census says that Jubal came to Canada in 1830, that he was married to Christina MacNaughton and that he and Christina, together with children Jubal, Alexander and Susan, were living with Jannet MacNaughton (Jubal' mother-in-law);  also in 1851, Jubal Watling   (F4243) petitioned for 50 acres of land on the South side of Black River adjoining south of William McBeath's meadow lot and east of a lot applied for by John Edge.  The petition is dated at Chatham on April 9, 1851 and includes his signature.  The property was vacant, surveyed but without improvements.  Other handwritten notes on the page are confusing in that they show apparent approval on May 21/1851 and Auction, August/51".  They are signed by Peters, the Deputy Surveyor.  No lot number is given but records show that a William MacBeath owned property in the area.

38.  JUBAL7 WATLING (CHRISTINA6 MACNAUGHTON, ALEXANDER5, JOHN4, MALCOLM3 MACNAUGHTAN, JOHN2 MACNAUCHTAN, MACNAUGHTON1 FAMILY)1 was born April 5, 1846 in The Meadows,1, and died February 16, 1915 in Black River1.  He married GRACE MACASKILL MACDONALD1 December 22, 1874 in Little Branch, daughter of JOHN MACDONALD and ELSPETH JARDINE.  She was born December 6, 18481, and died September 27, 1934 in Little Branch, NB1.


Notes for JUBAL WATLING:

Jubal Watling was granted 50 acres of land in the Meadows in 1884. In Aunt Janet Godfrey's tapes she said that her grandfather, Jubal , had a log house; she also said the her mother, Grace, did not want to move to Little Branch; 


As a younger man, he is believed to have worked for Senator Snowball.  The family decided in 1901 to move to Little Branch.  I suspect that they moved for a number of reasons.  Jubal's mother and father had both recently died, Grace's father had just died, her mother was not well, her brother, Jim, could not handle the farm, and the children would be much closer to school.  Aunt Tine and Aunt Elspie were married; the younger children would still be in school.


On November 13, 1901, James entered into an agreement with his sister, Grace and her husband, Jubal.  As parties to the agreement also were Jubal and Grace's four sons, George, John, James and Ernest.  A copy of this agreement can be found in the Northumberland County Registrar's office.  This transfer did not include the Jessie MacDonald property which Elmer Glendenning later bought.


All the land and buildings were turned over to the Watlings.  In return, the Watlings agreed to  "keep support, maintain and provide the said James J. McDonald and his mother, Mrs. John D. McDonald, each of them and the survivor of them during the respective terms of their natural lives with good and sufficient house, room, meat, drink, washing, clothing, bedding, fire, light, medicine, medical attendance, personal expenses and all such necessaries as persons of their ages and conditions in life usually require and are accustomed to."  The Watlings were also to provide a "decent Christian burial" paying all funeral expenses.


Post Office records show Jubal Watling as the Postmaster at Little Branch from December 1, 1903 to December 2, 1910.  Aunt Tine's name is not shown as Postmistress, although I believe that she kept the post office.  If this is true, it is likely that she ran the Post Office for her father between 1902, when Tom Adams died and 1907, when she married John Mackenzie.  


The Chatham Gazette of February 22, 1915 reported the following:


"The funeral of the late Jubal Watling was held on Thursday afternoon from his late residence, service started at 2:00 o'clock and being conducted by Rev. George Grant of Black River.  The choir of St. Stephen's Church, Black River, was present and rendered appropriate hymns.  Interment was in St. Stephens burying ground and the pallbearers were Messr. Alex Watling, William Watling, Donald Watling and Malcolm Watling, brothers of the deceased and John MacRae and Matthew Dickson, brothers-in-law.  The attendance at the service was very Large, those paying last tribute of respect to the deceased coming from miles around and testifying to their feeling of regret at the demise of a fine and widely respected citizen." 


Mildred MacKay remembered visiting her grandfather's home at the Meadows.




More About JUBAL WATLING:

Burial: Black River1


Notes for GRACE MACASKILL MACDONALD:

Married by Rev. Samuel Russell at bride's home, Little Branch.


The Tri- Weekly (Chatham, NB) included the following item for September 28, 1934.


"Many friends in Little Branch and vicinity heard with regret of the death of Mrs. Jubal Watling, which occurred at her home yesterday afternoon.

The late Mrs Watling was aged 86 years and before her marriage was Miss Grace MacDonald of Black River.

The funeral will be held on Saturday afternoon Service will commence at 2:30 o'clock and will be conducted by Rev. A. R. Gibson of Chatham.


Surviving are four daughters and four sons, Mrs. J. R MacKenzie, Loggieville; Mrs. J. W. MacNaughton, Mrs. J. R. Godfrey, Mrs. Elmer Glendenning, Black River; George and John, New Hampshire, James A. with whom she resided; Ernest Watling, Loggieville."


In my obsession is the following undated clipping:



"MRS. JUBAL WATLING


The funeral of the late Mrs. Jubal Watling took place from her late residence Saturday afternoon and was largely attended.  The service was conducted by Rev. A. R. Gibson of Chatham assisted by Rev. J. I. Morrison, of Black River, and the Rev. J. E. Forbes of Loggieville.  Mrs. F. A. Fowlie rendered a solo ‘Go Bury Thy sorrow.'  The eighth chapter of Romans was read as Mrs. Watling had requested.  ‘The Lord's My Shepherd' and ‘Safe in the Arms of Jesus,' favourite hymns of the deceased were sung.


The floral offerings were many and beautiful.


The pallbearers were Norman MacKenzie, Walter Glendenning, Gordon MacNaughton, Alex Newton (grandson), Jubal Dickson 

and James Jardine.


Interment was made in St. Stephen's cemetery.


Mrs. Watling was a lifelong Presbyterian, a sincere Christian, woman whose kingdom was her home and her children rise up to call her blessed.  Her gentle kindly ways endeared her to all who knew her and she will be greatly missed in the community."


I am told that she travelled to Niagara Falls in 1924 by automobile; believed to have died from dysentery (the summer complain)".

58.  JOHN ARCHIBALD WARD7 MACNAUGHTON (JOHN WILLIAM6, MALCOLM5, JOHN4, MALCOLM3 MACNAUGHTAN, JOHN2 MACNAUCHTAN, MACNAUGHTON1 FAMILY)1 was born 1872, and died 1958.  He married ELSPETH JARDINE WATLING1 August 30, 1899 in Black River, Rev John, Robertson1, daughter of JUBAL WATLING and GRACE MACDONALD.  She was born April 29, 1878 in Upper Black River, New Brunswick, Canada1, and died April 9, 19431.


Notes for JOHN ARCHIBALD WARD MACNAUGHTON:


Woods surveyor, farmer, operated a sawmill.  He and his brother-in-law, Elmer Glendenning, operated a "rosser" for peeling pulpwood.


Mourn Death of Well Known Resident


Black River (Special) - The death of John W. MacNaughton in his 86th year removed from the Miramichi scene a widely known and highly respected farmer and lumberman who, during his lifetime, took an active interest in affairs of his community.


For many years he worked with the A. and R. Loggie Company of Loggieville.  He was a lumberman, surveyor and cruiser of timber lands.  His work carried him far afield and he won many friends.  Until recent years he conducted his own mill and lumber business in Black River.


An active Liberal, he was party chairman for his district for a number of years.  He was a member of the county council and represented his native parish of Glenelg for 37 years and during one period was county warden.  He was also a former member of the board of Miramichi Hospital, Newcastle.


An able supporter of St. Stephen's United Church, he had been an elder of the church since 1926.  In 1907 he was chairman of the building committee for the present church and until recently was manager of the cemetery improvement committee.


Mr. MacNaughton was a member of Miramichi Lodge No. 18 F. and A.M., True Blue LOL No. 90, Unity Lodge No 18 IOOF and the Highland Society of N. B. at Miramichi.


He served as captain in the 73rd Northumberland Regiment for a number of years prior to the First World War.


Mr. MacNaughton was one of a family of 10 sons and three daughters of the late John and Margaret Ward MacNaughton, who lived in a hewed log dwelling on the present homestead, one of the first settled in Black River.


His wife, Elspeth, daughter of Grace and Jubal Watling, predeceased him in 1943.


Surviving are eight daughters and three sons, 22 grandchildren, six great grandchildren and three brothers.



More About JOHN ARCHIBALD WARD MACNAUGHTON:

Date born 2: May 15, 1872, Black River, New Brunswick1

Died 2: January 6, 1958, Black River, N. B.1

Burial: January 9, 1958, Black River1

Occupation: Farmer, sawmill operator, woods surveyor,

Religion: United Church


Notes for ELSPETH JARDINE WATLING:

I have an newspaper clipping, dated only 1943, which reads as follows:


FUNERAL SERVICE HELD AT CHATHAM Family and friends of Mrs. John W MacNaughton were shocked when her death occurred suddenly on Friday following a brief illness.  She was  formerly Miss Elspeth J. Watling, daughter of the late Jubal and Grace Watling and was widely known throughout the community as a woman of kindly character held in high regard.  Always interested in the church work of St. Stephens United Church she had a life membership in the Women's Missionary Society and was an active member in many organizations.


She is survived by her husband, John, W. MacNaughton; three sons, John, Gardiner, Me; Gordon, Dalhousie, and Flt. Sgt. Malcolm, RCAF, Ottawa; eight daughters, Mrs Archibald MacNaughton, Black River Bridge; Mrs James Edge, Mrs Harold MacKay and Miss Luella, all of Cambridge, Mass; Mrs Clifford MacNaughton, Gardiner, Me; Mrs A W Lewis, Chatham; Mrs Elliot Cail, Pine Ridge, NB; and Mrs Perley McKnight, New Jersey, NB; three sisters, Mrs. John K. MacKenzie, Loggieville; Mrs. E. A Glendenning and Mrs John R Godfrey, Black River Bridge; four brothers, James A Watling, Black River Bridge; Ernest D Watling, Loggieville; John M Watling, Boston, and J. George Watling, New Hampshire.

The funeral was held this afternoon with service at the house conducted by Rev. Peter Jackson and at St. Stephen's Church.  Interment was in the adjacent cemetery.  Numerous floral tributes were received.

Notes for MALCOLM JAMES MACNAUGHTON:

MacNaughton, Malcolm J.  - It is with great sorrow that we announce the passing of Malcolm, peacefully at the Aurora Rest Haven Nursing Home February 17, 2005 in his 86th year. Predeceased last year by his loving wife of 63 years, Mrytle and leaving devoted sons John, Brian, Robert and his wife Linda. 

Cherished grandfather to James, Laura, Kevin, Derek, Dean, Ashley and Brittany and great grandfather to Hailey. 

Predeceased by sisters, Grace, Margaret, Gladys, Mildred, Ruth, Jean, Ernestine and Luella and brothers John and Gordon. 


He will be sadly missed by his many nieces and nephews across Canada and the United States as well as many dear friends.


Veteran of W.W. II, RCAF stationed at Uplands Base, Ottawa and at Comox, B.C.

A self made business man who founded and operated a successful electrical contracting business for many years and a Past President of the Electrical Contractors Association.


Relatives and friends will be received at the Ogden Funeral Home, 4164 Sheppard Ave East, from 2- 4 and 7-9 Tuesday February 22, 2005. Service at Victoria Park United Church, 1 Stamford Square North, Scarborough Wednesday February 23, 2005 at 11:00 AM followed by internment at Highland Memorial Gardens.


Donations, if desired in lieu of flowers, to Victoria Park United Church, Scarborough or St. Stephens United Church, Black River Bridge, New Brunswick would be greatly appreciated.

80.  CHRISTINA CATHERINE8 WATLING (JUBAL7, CHRISTINA6 MACNAUGHTON, ALEXANDER5, JOHN4, MALCOLM3 MACNAUGHTAN, JOHN2 MACNAUCHTAN, MACNAUGHTON1 FAMILY)1 was born February 3, 1876 in Upper Black River, New Brunswick, Canada1, and died September 22, 19561.  She married (1) ROBERT THOMAS ADAMS1 August 17, 1895, son of JAMES ADAMS and ELLEN FERRIS.  He was born July 28, 1872 in Margate, PEI, Canada1, and died September 29, 1902 in Little Branch, NB1.  She married (2) JOHN K MACKENZIE1 August 19071, son of JOHN MACKENZIE and CATHERINE MACDONALD.  He was born 1850 in Cape Breton, NS, and died April 5, 1925 in Loggieville, NB.


Notes for CHRISTINA CATHERINE WATLING:

Aunt Tine was the oldest of the family.  She married Tom Adams but he died in 1902 at age 30.  Aunt Tine was then 26.  Tom Adams was thought to have died of some rare disease and was living with his in-laws, Grace and Jubal Watling, at the time.


Post Office records show Jubal Watling as the Postmaster at Little Branch from December 1, 1903 to December 2, 1910.  Aunt Tine's name is not shown as Postmistress, although we believe that she lived in the Jessie MacDonald home (where Norman Glendenning now lives) and kept the post office.  If this is true, it is likely that she ran the Post Office for her father between 1902, when Tom Adams died and 1907 when she married John Mackenzie.  John MacKenzie moved to the Miramichi from Cape Breton to work in the ship building industry


After her marriage to John MacKenzie, they moved to the W. S. Loggie home and farm just east of Loggieville.  John MacKenzie is thought to have died about 1924.  Sometime after that, Aunt Tine moved next to the church in Loggieville.  Later she sold her home and moved to Mount St. Joseph's where she was living when she died in 1956.  John MacKenzie is believed to have come to the Miramichi to work in ship building and, Edith told me, he was related to the MacKenzies who lived up the Branch at the MacKenzie Settlement.


Jean MacKenzie, was severely disabled with rheumatoid  arthritis for many years and lived with her mother in Loggieville.  When Aunt Tine was no longer able to look after her, Jean moved to Chippewa, Ontario with her half-brother, Willard, and later to a nursing home.


Hartley, the son of James Adams and Ella Watling, was adopted by Gertrude Chase but was really raised by Aunt Tine.  Hartley served in WW2 and later moved, first to England and later to Australia where he died sometime in the 1990's.


I have an undated press clipping which reads as follows:


The death of Mrs. Christina MacKenzie, wife of the late John MacKenzie, a well-known resident of Loggieville, occurred at Mount St. Joseph's Pavilion here at an early hour Friday morning.


The late Mrs MacKenzie was the former Christina Watling of Black River and was eighty years of age.  She was twice married, her first husband being Thomas Adams of Black River.


Surviving from her first marriage are two sons, James Adams and Willard Adams both of Chippewa, Ontario.

Surviving from her second marriage are two daughters, Mrs. F. Schickle, Mandarin, Florida and Miss Jean MacKenzie, Chippewa, Ontario.  Two sisters and three brothers also survive.  They are Mrs. John R. Godfrey, Black River Bridge, and Mrs Elmer Glendenning, Black River; James A Watling, Black River, George A. Watling, New Hampshire, and D. Ernest Watling, Loggieville.  


The funeral was held on Sunday afternoon from the home of Ernest Watling, Loggieville.  A short service at the house was followed by a public service at Knox United Church.  Interment was made in the Pine Grove Cemetery.


More About CHRISTINA CATHERINE WATLING:

Burial: Pine Grove Cem, Loggieville, New Brunswick, Canada1

Religion: United Church

Notes for JOHN K MACKENZIE:

Aunt Janet's tapes say the Aunt Tine and John Mackenzie lived for a while in Cape Breton but moved back and bought a place in Loggieville, (near the end of the McKnight road); Joan Mackenzie wrote: John MacKenzie used to say, " I had 3, she had 3 and we had 3'. He was referring to the children they had; Marjorie Young also thinks that they moved to Cape Breton to work, then returned to Loggieville to work in Loggie's mill;  Barry MacKenzie says "Some in our family recall that there was some connection between our MacKenzies and John K. MacKenzie, and given that our ancestors originally settled at Pictou, this could quite possibly be the case."

Notes for ELSPETH JARDINE WATLING:

I have an newspaper clipping, dated only 1943, which reads as follows:


FUNERAL SERVICE HELD AT CHATHAM Family and friends of Mrs. John W MacNaughton were shocked when her death occurred suddenly on Friday following a brief illness.  She was  formerly Miss Elspeth J. Watling, daughter of the late Jubal and Grace Watling and was widely known throughout the community as a woman of kindly character held in high regard.  Always interested in the church work of St. Stephens United Church she had a life membership in the Women's Missionary Society and was an active member in many organizations.


She is survived by her husband, John, W. MacNaughton; three sons, John, Gardiner, Me; Gordon, Dalhousie, and Flt. Sgt. Malcolm, RCAF, Ottawa; eight daughters, Mrs Archibald MacNaughton, Black River Bridge; Mrs James Edge, Mrs Harold MacKay and Miss Luella, all of Cambridge, Mass; Mrs Clifford MacNaughton, Gardiner, Me; Mrs A W Lewis, Chatham; Mrs Elliot Cail, Pine Ridge, NB; and Mrs Perley McKnight, New Jersey, NB; three sisters, Mrs. John K. MacKenzie, Loggieville; Mrs. E. A Glendenning and Mrs John R Godfrey, Black River Bridge; four brothers, James A Watling, Black River Bridge; Ernest D Watling, Loggieville; John M Watling, Boston, and J. George Watling, New Hampshire.

The funeral was held this afternoon with service at the house conducted by Rev. Peter Jackson and at St. Stephen's Church.  Interment was in the adjacent cemetery.  Numerous floral tributes were received.


More About ELSPETH JARDINE WATLING:

Burial: Black River Cem, New Brunswick, Canada1

Religion: United Church


Notes for JOHN ARCHIBALD WARD MACNAUGHTON:


Woods surveyor, farmer, operated a sawmill.  He and his brother-in-law, Elmer Glendenning, operated a "rosser" for peeling pulpwood.


Mourn Death of Well Known Resident


Black River (Special) - The death of John W. MacNaughton in his 86th year removed from the Miramichi scene a widely known and highly respected farmer and lumberman who, during his lifetime, took an active interest in affairs of his community.


For many years he worked with the A. and R. Loggie Company of Loggieville.  He was a lumberman, surveyor and cruiser of timber lands.  His work carried him far afield and he won many friends.  Until recent years he conducted his own mill and lumber business in Black River.


An active Liberal, he was party chairman for his district for a number of years.  He was a member of the county council and represented his native parish of Glenelg for 37 years and during one period was county warden.  He was also a former member of the board of Miramichi Hospital, Newcastle.


An able supporter of St. Stephen's United Church, he had been an elder of the church since 1926.  In 1907 he was chairman of the building committee for the present church and until recently was manager of the cemetery improvement committee.


Mr. MacNaughton was a member of Miramichi Lodge No. 18 F. and A.M., True Blue LOL No. 90, Unity Lodge No 18 IOOF and the Highland Society of N. B. at Miramichi.


He served as captain in the 73rd Northumberland Regiment for a number of years prior to the First World War.


Mr. MacNaughton was one of a family of 10 sons and three daughters of the late John and Margaret Ward MacNaughton, who lived in a hewed log dwelling on the present homestead, one of the first settled in Black River.


His wife, Elspeth, daughter of Grace and Jubal Watling, predeceased him in 1943.


Surviving are eight daughters and three sons, 22 grandchildren, six great grandchildren and three brothers.

Burial: January 9, 1958, Black River1

Occupation: Farmer, sawmill operator, woods surveyor,

82.  JUBAL GEORGE8 WATLING (JUBAL7, CHRISTINA6 MACNAUGHTON, ALEXANDER5, JOHN4, MALCOLM3 MACNAUGHTAN, JOHN2 MACNAUCHTAN, MACNAUGHTON1 FAMILY)1 was born May 22, 1881 in Upper Black River, New Brunswick, Canada1, and died September 1958 in Orange City,Jacksonville, Florida, USA1.  He married EMMA BEATRICE RONAN1 June 21, 1905 in Woodstock, New Brunswick, daughter of WILLIAM RONAN and SARAH OLDFIELD.  She was born May 14, 1876 in Redbank, New Brunswick1, and died 1950 in Newport, New Hampshire, USA1.


Notes for JUBAL GEORGE WATLING:

In 1908 was working in Portland; believe that he worked for Elmer Glendenning while in Portland and likely in the New Hampshire woods; Edith MacKenzie thought that Uncle George also worked in the South with Elmer Glendenning; in later life he worked in maintenance at Dartmouth College (?), in New Hampshire.


His son, Elbridge, told me that Uncle George worked in construction work in Augusta, Georgia and during WW1, worked at Langley Field (that would likely be when Dad was there).  Although he was making good money, he left Virginia by boat for New York and then by train to New Brunswick.  (Apparently, they removed his trunk from the train at the NB border)  He stayed for 13 weeks with his brother, Jim.  Uncle Jim gave him a horse so he could visit around with his relatives.  Papers provided by Tina Newton state that he returned to Canada in 1919 for four months.


He worked for a couple of years on a dam on the Connecticut river at 15 Mile Falls on the Vermont/New Hampshire border (Wilder Dam? near Hanover, NH).  The face of the dam was 103 feet high, one of the highest ever at the time.  As I recall, Elbridge went to school there.


Granddaughter, Christina. says he was married in USA, probably in Hampton, Va.  because his wife's family had moved there from Redbank, NB.  She also said that he was working on the Tennessee Valley Dam Project at the time his son, Elbridge, was born.  Technically, the Tennessee Valley Authority was not formed until much later, however, the dams on the Ocoee River (where Dad worked) became part of the TVA.  The Ocoee River originates in or near Georgia.  It is likely that  possible, for example, that Aunt Emma went to Augusta when her son was born and Mum came to New Brunswick.  Uncle George also worked at Langley Field in Virginia.


Uncle George did not return to Black River for 31 years, and then came every year, sometimes with his grand children.  Later he moved to a lodge operated by the Order of the Moose near Jacksonville, Florida, and died there of pneumonia.


When his sister Jane went to Portland in 1908, I believe she stayed for a while with her brother George.  I recall her telling that she arrived early in the morning and Uncle George was supposed to meet her.  He did not show.  Later she learned that he had come to the station but did not recognize her.  She had grown since he had left Black River.  


His granddaughter, Tina Watling Newton sent copies of papers including:


An Application for a Certificate of Arrival: dated April 16, 1931 at 13 Cheney Street, Newport, New Hampshire, stating that he had entered the United States at Vanceboro, Mine in September, 1903; he had bought his ticket at Chatham, travelled to Saint John, the immigration officer came aboard at Saint John and processed passengers; He was heading for Bangor, Maine and was joining "no one' in the USA.  His application gave his occupation as labour foremen and construction work.  Alfred H. MacLean,of Little Branch was travelling with him.

Another paper shows him being discharged from the National Guard of the State of Maine in May 1908.  It was an honorable discharge and his CO said that he would be glad to have him back.  He had only enlisted on March 21, 1908 but apparently had moved out of state.

85.  JAMES ALEXANDER WILLIAM8 WATLING (JUBAL7, CHRISTINA6 MACNAUGHTON, ALEXANDER5, JOHN4, MALCOLM3 MACNAUGHTAN, JOHN2 MACNAUCHTAN, MACNAUGHTON1 FAMILY)1 was born January 15, 1888 in The Meadows1, and died November 25, 1983 in Little Branch, NB1.  He married KATHERINE CAMERON MILLS1 July 16, 1917 in MacNaughton residence, Little Branch, daughter of JOHN MILLS and MARY MACNAUGHTON.  She was born December 9, 1890 in Hardwicke, New Brunswick, Canada1, and died January 14, 1965 in Black River Brid, New Brunswick, Canada1.


Notes for JAMES ALEXANDER WILLIAM WATLING:

Ken thinks that Uncle Jim was not well one winter and moved up with Dad in the woods of New Hampshire where he was a "cookee".  His father died in 1915 and he returned from Portland to look after his mother; he had worked in either a chemists shop or a photo shop.  May have been engaged while in Portland.  One story has it that Uncle Jim gave a "diamond" to a girl in Portland prior to his returning home.  Uncle Jim was in the militia anw went to camp in Sussex.


I believe that his father left the home place jointly to sons Jim and Ernest and Jim bought out the other half.    A newspaper clipping reported the following:


`James A Watling of Black River Bridge died Friday, November 25, 1983 at the Hotel Dieu Hospital, Chatham after a lengthy illness.  Born in Black River Bridge, he was the son of the late Jubal and Grace (MacDonald) Watling.


He had been a farmer and, for 42 years, a smelt buyer for W S Loggie Co of Chatham.


He was also the first leader of the 4H club in the area, , a charter member and board member of the former Newcastle Creamery, now Northumberland Co-Op.  He was also a member of St. Pauls Presbyterian Church, Black River Bridge, an elder of the church for 62 years and treasurer of the Presbytery for some time.  His wife, the former Kathryn Mills died in 1965.

He is survived by his daughter, Mrs Alvin Young (Marjorie) of Loggieville; four sons, Winfield, Roy and George all of Moncton, and Neil Watling with whom he resided; 16 grandchildren and by nine great-grandchildren.  The body rested at Adams Funeral Home, Chatham.  The funeral was held at 2 pm Mon. 86.  JANE ELIZABETH JARDINE8 WATLING (JUBAL7, CHRISTINA6 MACNAUGHTON, ALEXANDER5, JOHN4, MALCOLM3 MACNAUGHTAN, JOHN2 MACNAUCHTAN, MACNAUGHTON1 FAMILY)1 was born February 28, 1890 in Upper Black River, NB1, and died March 25, 1964 in Newcastle Hospital, NB1.  She married ELMER ALTON GLENDENNING1 October 18, 1910 in Bride's home, Little Branch, Black River, New Brunswick1, son of WALTER GLENDENNING and ELIZABETH PETERS.  He was born June 29, 1875 in Canobie, NB1, and died May 18, 1938 in Black River Bridge, NB1.


Notes for JANE ELIZABETH JARDINE WATLING:

Jane was the second youngest of a family of eight born to Jubal Watling and Grace (MacAskill) MacDonald, born, presumably at home, at the Meadows, Upper Black River, New Brunswick.  She was named after Jane Elizabeth Jardine , daughter of John Jardine, (1836-1912)who married Donald Cameron (Cameron Russell's grandmother)


When she was about 10 years old, the family moved from the Meadows to Little Branch.  She attended the Upper Black River school and later the school at Little Branch. 


Jane arrived in Portland on January 24, 1908 just prior to her 18th birthday.   Jane arrived in the early morning of January 24, 1908 and waited for her brother, George, to meet her. Apparently he came to the station but did not recognize her.   She had grown up while he was away from Black River.  Her brother Jack also worked in Portland at that time and likely there were many cousins. We also know that her brother Jim worked in Portland in a chemist/pharmacy, and also in photography, but whether he was there at that time we do not know.  


Among Mum's possessions was an envelope, addressed in Dad's handwriting and mailed from Wisconsin, showing Mum's address as 130 Emery Street, Portland.


She married Elmer Glendenning on October 18, 1910 and returned to the US where they lived and worked for the next nine years; .Information about this period is found in the notes about Elmer.


In Black river, Jane was active in her church, the Women's Missionary society and the Women's league.  She was also a long time member of the women's institute.  She liked to read and, in the early years at least, wrote poetry.


After 28 years of marriage, Jane Elizabeth Jardine (Watling) became a widow in 1939; she was 48 at the time.  The two oldest daughters, Grace and Venetia, were married and living elsewhere; her daughter, Lillian, was living and working in the Boston area.  Elinor and the five boys, the youngest being nine years old, were living at home.


The next decade, the wartime years, brought a number of significant changes to the community.  The commencement of classes, and the construction of the Miramichi Rural High School, allowed young people of the community to continue their schooling to the end of high school while continuing to live at home; before this, the nearest high school was in Loggieville, eleven miles away.  The looming war resulted in the construction of an airbase at Chatham, a radar station at St. Margaret's, and a bombing range on the road to Loggieville.  These developments meant a significant growth of non-farm jobs for the community.  The outbreak of hostilities also soon resulted in sons, sons-in-law, neighbours, relatives and friends entering the armed forces.  The decade also brought improved roads, regular bus service to Chatham and electric lights.  By the end of the decade, Black River had moved from a barter to a cash economy.


Although the sons continued to operate the family farm for the next decade, the decade also brought significant changes to the family;  Walter finished building his house on the Dan MacLean property, married and moved out; Lillian returned to New Brunswick and married; Elinor, Kenneth, Norman and Don attended the new Miramichi High School; Walter, Kenneth and Norman served in the armed forces; John, after operating the farm during the war, married, built a house and moved to the Bob MacLean property; Elinor married and moved out; Kenneth returned from military service, married and moved away; Don finished high school and entered teaching; Norman married and settled on the home place.    


Jane continued to be active in the community generally and encouraged her family and others to do the same.  She was especially active in her church, the Women's Missionary Society, the Presbyterian League and the Women's Institute.  She believed in education and supported the construction of a high school for the area; she also encouraged her family and others to continue their schooling. (It is interesting to note that the Miramichi Rural High School was the first rural high school in the Province; immediately following WW2, the number grew rapidly with, I believe, federal financial support, until there were approximately 50 rural high schools throughout the New Brunswick.)   


Norman and Jean married in 1951 and Mum continued to live with them until she died in 1964. Mum died in the Miramichi Hospital of cancer of the pancreas having been in the hospital only about two weeks.  


The following vignettes are intended to round out the picture of Mother, her interests, activities and passions:


1 While discussing VE Day with Vance MacNaughton in June of 1995 at the shopping mall in Douglastown, he told Ken and I about his memory of VE Day (May 8, 1944).  As readers will know, the Little Branch School was down the road and across the brook from home.  Vance was a student in the school at the time when, suddenly, there was a knock and, without waiting for a response, the door flew open the school door flew open and Mum announced to teacher and students alike "The War is over".  

2 Mother had no formal training as a nurse but made herself available and was frequently called upon by others in the community in time of need such as for a birth or death in a family.  

3 Her " pin money" came from trading wool for yarn.  The wool was wash in rainwater heated on the kitchen stove, carried to the front yard, washed and rinsed several times and spread on the grass to dry then gathered up and bagged.  down into the grass and, therefore, being difficult to gather and bag when dry. The bags wool were loaded into the Model A Ford and taken to Brown's Woolen Mills in Nelson where it would be made into yarn.  She bartered her wool for skeins of yarn in colours, but mostly in white or grey. She knitted mitts and socks for the family, and sometimes for Christmas gifts, and sold surplus yarn to neighbours and, I think, she had some customers by mail.


4 Bread was a staple and a large family required many loaves of bread each week.  The recipe was mixed in a large, round blue-enamelled, bread pan.  A cake of Fleischmann's yeast was "started" in a cup of warm water.  The dough was made into loaves, set in a warm place to rise, and then put in the oven to bake.  My recollection is that she would make 7 loaves at a time twice a week.  


5 Mother loved to have visitors.  There were a half-dozen at every meal and putting up a meal for 15 after church on Sunday, or any other day, did not faze her a bit or if it did, she did not let on.  I cannot ever even once remember her saying, I wish they hadn't or wouldn't come today.  Meals were not fancy but they were always tasty.  Of course, when the visitors were family, the daughters or daughters-in-law, pitched in to help.


6 Oil lamps were tended early each day so that, when night fell, there was no scramble for a light.  We had three oil lamps plus, in later years, an Aladdin lamp.  Lamps were filled with kerosene, wicks were trimmed, globes were wiped first with an old newspaper to remove the soot, then washed, dried and replaced.  When finished, the kerosene lights were placed on a shelf beside the clock high on the kitchen wall.   The Aladdin lamp used gas, naphtha?, and did not require such daily care. 


7 Securing food for the coming winter was a major concern in a household.  While Mother had no special role in the planting or harvesting, she played the major role in pressuring food for winter.  The first step was to have a good garden, which resulted in potatoes, carrots and turnips stored in bins in the basement.  Carrots were a bit more complex and were usually stored in sand in the basement.  Apples were stored in a cold bedroom, usually the large bedroom over the driveway.  Some preserves were put up - peaches, cucumber pickles, chow and mustard beans.  Several baskets of peaches were purchased and these were bottled along with some strawberries, blueberries and maybe even raspberries.  


St. Stephen's Presbyterian Church annual report for 1910 includes the following:

 

"GLENDENNING-WATLING - At the home of the bride's parents, on October 18, Alton Elmer Glendenning of Bathurst and Jane Elizabeth Watling of Little Branch".

Godfrey, Black River, and two brothers, James A. Watling, Black River and Ernest Watling, Loggieville; 27 grandchildren and seven great grandchildren.

Notes for ELMER ALTON GLENDENNING:

Elmer, the second child and first son to Walter and Elizabeth (Peters) Glendenning, was born in Canobie on land that is now a grove of trees directly across the road from the Canobie School, now the Women's Institute Hall.   Baptismal record shows birthdate as June 10, 1875


It is likely that he went to the one room school on a corner of his Uncle's farm next door since the family seems to have valued education; this is not surprising in that his grandmother was a teacher and lived in the home until Elmer was 20.  As further evidence, his brother, Chip took a business course, Guy studied but did not finish dentistry and Margaret trained as a nurse.  It is likely that Dad took some technical courses while in Portland; I remember some engineering books at home.  His oldest sister, Venetia, provided a home for his sister, Margaret, so that she could finish high school in Portland; I believe that Uncle Guy also completed high school there; maybe others did as well.    


Elmer's father was a farmer and, judging by the amount of land accumulated, a successful one at that.  Elmer, and all the boys, and maybe even the girls, would be expected to do their share.  The land would have to be cleared of trees, and those who know Canobie, will remember the stony fields; and all the work would be done by hand or with the aid of horses.


At that time he probably knew people, even friends, who went to Maine or New Hampshire during the winter to work in the woods and then return to farm and fish in the summer; he may have even done that himself.  


The Glendennings were well established in Portland in the first decade of the 1900s. Frank moved there in 1898 and Elmer by 1901. Venetia, the oldest child, seems to have played the role of "host" as each new family member moved south.  Venetia lived in Falmouth Foreside which is between Portland and Freeport.  Other siblings known to have lived in Portland, at least for a while, include Margaret, Raymond and Guy.   In addition, Maude and her husband, Jim Cormier lived in Lincoln, New Hampshire where Uncle Jim Cormier worked in the mill, and Leonard and his family lived in Maine.  We also know, for example, that his Aunt, Mary Ann (Harley) who had married a Stevens and lived next door in Canobie, had moved to Portland to be with family after her husband died in 1870.   Land grant records show that Lot # 28 in Canobie, 100 acres, was granted to Atton E. Glendenning on 1901-07-06.  .


Prior to his marriage in 1910, we know that Elmer, his brother, Frank, and likely brothers Ray and Guy operated a trucking business under the name Glendenning Bros. & Co. (All Kinds of Team Work) at 24 Hammond Street. They had horses and wagons and did all kinds of hauling.  Portland was the winter terminus of the Great Northern Railway, later the CNR, which meant that much goods would be moved, in all kinds of combinations, from trains, ships to warehouses, retail stores and homes.  One role Dad told about was that of delivering ice to homes. Elmer also had crews of men and horses in the woods in  winter, we believe in both Maine and new Hampshire, but possibly also in Vermont.  Other people from Black River worked with him including his brother-in-law, James A. Watling  and Ramsay MacLean (a nephew of Adam MacLean who was married to Elmer's first cousin. Millie.)  According to Herbert Daley, Herb Daley, Dad's first cousin, also worked in the woods with Elmer.  


In the first decade of the 20th century, Elmer and his brothers operated a cartage business in Portland, Maine.  I believe that his brother, Frank, was a partner in the business and others may have been as well.  Bobby Glendenning believes said that his father, Guy, worked in the business and brother Ray worked with them as a blacksmith.  By 1910, Elmer was working on the Rothschild dam in Wausau, Wisconsin from where, on January 25, 1910, he mailed "Jeannie Glendenning" in Portland; some dams were being built to improve the rafting of logs; others were built for the generation of power.  


Elmer and Jane were married in Litle Branch in 1910 and returned to Portland, however, by February 1911 they were living in Parksville, Tennessee where Elmer worked on Ocoee #1, the first of three dams build on the Ocoee River. Later Mum and Dad moved up the Ocoee River to Caney Creek where a second dam was built.  (This project included the construction of a flume line to carry water for five  miles over valleys and along rocky ledges to a head pond with a drop of 250 feet to the turbines below.)  The flume line is still in existence and designated as a national historic site.  Over the next few years they moved from construction site to construction site in Hampton, Virginia, Mussel Shoals. Alabama, North haven, Connecticut,   Dad also worked for a while in Abiline, Texas but I do not believe that Mum moved there.   Probably the other major project was in Virginia where Elmer was in charge of the clearing of Langley Field which, I believe, handled both sea and land based airplanes.  It was a defense establishment and, I believe they lived on th site; people needed passes when returning to the site; Venetia's pass was for 1918, I do not know the year of Lillian's pass.  Dad worked in Mussell Shoals likely either at the Wilson Dam or at the nearby nitrate plant.  At the same time, we have evidence that Uncle Frank worked at Plant No. 1, US Nitrate Co. in Sheffield Alabama.  He was working  for the J. G. White Engineering Co, which is the same one Dad worked for in Hampton, Virginia.


On July 21, 1914, Elmer bought the Jessie MacDonald property at Little Branch  and they move there during late 1913 or early 1914  (The property passed from Jessie to her nephew, Alexader from whom Dad made the purchase)   Elmer beame post master in 1914 but resigned in 1916 and moved to Fairhaven, Connecticut where they lived at 3 Perkins Street.  By 1919, they took up permanent residence at their home in Little Branch.  Over the years he purchased a number of other properties, one for each of his sons.


Like others in Black River, they operated a mixed farm that included cows, pigs, hens, geese, horses, turkeys; crops included a farm garden, oats, barley, wheat, buckwheat, and hay.  Cash was scarce; most of their business was accomplished thriugh barter of eggs, chickens,, beef, pork, the sale of weaners (Piglets four to six weeks old), geese, turkeys, ducks, bales of hay, bags of grain, cream, potatoes, furnace wood, and butter, etc.  In addition, the  farm ensured that the family had meat and potatoes for their own use.  For a while he had a team of oxen which he used around the farm.  Lillian remembers using them to haul sap from back on the "ridge".


Elmer active in the community including the building of St. Paul's Presbyterian Church (on June 14, 1926 was elected to the first Board of Managers) , was a prime mover in the construction of a community hall.  The first hall was opened in 1931 and burned to the ground in 1932.  A second hall was built in 1933.  He was active in the Orange Lodge, Oddfellows, and Masons.


In addition to farming, he and his brother-in-law, John W. MacNaughton operated a pulp business. They operated a "rosser", a machine for peeling pulp in Middle Black River, across from the Cameron School, and operated lumber camps in St. Margaret's and the Black River area.  He continued to be active in construction and built the bridge at John Godfrey's and John Dick's. His last job away from home was the construction of Tinker Dam near Woodstock, NB


Elmer Glendenning was known as a hard worker. He built and repaired his own harness, re-soled the children's shoes, and made axe handles. Even when he was not able to work, Brydone Weeks told that Dad had himself taken to the field in a wheelbarrow where he sharpened the scythes while the men were cutting marsh hay.  Dad suffered for a number of years with pernicious anaemia (or was it leukemia ?).  Walter remembers the wagon rack breaking one evening when coming from the woods with a load of wood.  Later he and Dad returned to the woods, unloaded the wood, cut poles, repaired the wagon rack, and returned home so that the wagon load of wood was ready to be taken to Loggieville the next day.  Part of the repair work was carried out by the light of a lantern.  Lillian remembers Dad up on the roof shingling at daybreak.  


Dad was a founding member of an Agricultural Society (#103) and was its Secretary Treasurer for fourteen years.  An Agricultural Society, and there were many throughout the Province,  was like a Co-op.  Farmers banded together and bought seeds, lime and fertilizer, etc. in bulk and sold it to themselves


It is interesting that Dad's name sometimes appears as Alton Elmer and other times as Elmer Alton Glendenning.  Although I have not seen a birth certificate, my earliest references are to Elmer Alton Glendenning.  


Venetia's clippings included the following believed to have been taken from the Moncton Times:


"CHATHAM,May 22 - The community of Black River and the Miramichi generally will learn with deep regret of the death of Elmer A. Glendenning at his home Black River Bridge.  For 14 years he had been the energetic secretary-treasurer of the progressive Agricultural Society of that district.  For over a quarter of a century  he has been a member of the Masonic Order; for 30 years enjoyed membership in the Oddfellows Craft, and for the long period of 43 years was a member of the Loyal Orange Lodge.

Surviving are his wife (formerly Miss. Jean Watling of Black River and nine children.  There are four daughters, Mrs. Roy Smith of Medford, Mass., Mrs Cameron Russell of Point Aux Carr, Lillian of Needham, Mass., and Elinor at home, an infant daughter, Margaret Virginia was buried in Hampton, Virginia in 1918; five sons, Walter, John, Kenneth, Norman and Don, all at home.  

The funeral of the late Mr. Glendenning was held yesterday afternoon at 2:30 o'clock with Dr. Genge of Calvin Church, Chatham, officiating.  Interment was made in St. Stephen's Cemetery, Black River."

87.  DONALD ERNEST8 WATLING (JUBAL7, CHRISTINA6 MACNAUGHTON, ALEXANDER5, JOHN4, MALCOLM3 MACNAUGHTAN, JOHN2 MACNAUCHTAN, MACNAUGHTON1 FAMILY)1 was born February 23, 1892 in Upper Black River, New Brunswick, Canada1, and died December 29, 1974 in Loggieville1.  He married SARAH ALICE MACLEAN1 July 6, 19211, daughter of DONALD MACLEAN and CATHERINE FOWLIE.  She was born 1899, and died December 9, 19901.


Notes for DONALD ERNEST WATLING:

Prior to WW1 did construction work at Ocoee#1 in Parksville, Tennessee.  


Went overseas with the 26th. Battalion #70039); served in France; suffered head wounds at Manchy, France on August 27, 1918;  awarded the MM; 


After the war he moved to Niagara Falls, Ontario;  returned to Little Branch about 1933 and lived with brother, Jim and children attended Little Branch school; moved to Savoie's Beach in Loggieville; did security at Chatham air base during WW2; Employed at WS Loggie Company, Chatham until retirement.


The following clipping was in the local newspaper:


"The death of D. Ernest Watling Sr. of Manse Lane, Loggieville, occurred Sunday at Hotel Dieu, here, following a long illness.  He was 81.  Born at Black River Bridge, he was the son of the late Jubal and Grace (MacDonald) Watling.

He attended Knox United Church, Loggieville, and was a veteran of the First Worls War, serving overseas with the 26th Battallion.  During the Second World War he was a security guard at CFB Chatham.  He was a member of the Chatham Branch Royal Canadian Legion.  For a number of years the family resided in Niagara Falls and, following their return to the Miramichi, he worked for W S Loggie Company Limited here until his retirement some years ago.

He is survived by his wife, the former Alice MacLean of Black River Bridge; one daughter, Mrs Everett Wishart (Joyce) of Bathurst; three sons, Clifton (Kip) of Campbellton, Ernest of Buffalo, NY, Gilbert of Newcastle; one sister, Mrs. John r. Godfrey (Janet) of Black River Bridge; one brother, James A Watling also of Black River Bridge, and by 16 grandchildren.  The body is resting at his home in Loggieville.

The Funeral will be held at 2:30 pm Tuesday at Knox United Church, Loggieville, with entombment in Black River Memorial Vault to await burial in the spring.

103.  KATHERINE CAMERON8 MILLS (MARY7 MACNAUGHTON, JOHN6, DONALD5, JOHN4, MALCOLM3 MACNAUGHTAN, JOHN2 MACNAUCHTAN, MACNAUGHTON1 FAMILY)1 was born December 9, 1890 in Hardwicke, New Brunswick, Canada1, and died January 14, 1965 in Black River Brid, New Brunswick, Canada1.  She married JAMES ALEXANDER WILLIAM WATLING1 July 16, 1917 in MacNaughton residence, Little Branch, son of JUBAL WATLING and GRACE MACDONALD.  He was born January 15, 1888 in The Meadows1, and died November 25, 1983 in Little Branch, NB1.


Notes for KATHERINE CAMERON MILLS:

Taught school in Loggieville and boarded at Audrey Loggie's; also taught in (Rosetown?) Saskatchewan.  Both her father and mother died when she was young and Aunt Kate was raised by her mother's brother, Donald MacNaughton - next house above the Glendenning s.  Her brothers Roy and Archie, were raised by John Archie MacNaughton, John Archibald MacNaughton, married to Mary Cameron.  Mildred Mackay believes that Uncle Ern courted Aunt Kate before she and Uncle Jim were married.


The Moncton Times of January 14, 1965 reported the following:


"The death of Mrs. Katharine Cameron Watling, 74, wife of James A. Watling, of Black River Bridge, occurred at her home Thursday following a lengthy illness.

She was born at Hardwicke, a daughter of the late John and Mary (MacNaughton) Mills.

She was a member of St. Paul's Presbyterian Church, the Women's Missionary Society, and honourary president of the Presbyterial of the WMS.  for a number of years she was oranist and choir direcor at the church.

Mrs. Watling was also a member of the Black River Women's Institute.

Surviving are her husband, a daughhter, Mrs. Alvin Young (Marjorie), Loggieville; four sons, Winfield, Roy and George, Moncton, and Neil, Black River; a brother, Roy Mills, Calgary, Alts., and 15 grandchildren.

The body is resitng at  the home, where the funeral service will be held at a time to be announced."


More About KATHERINE CAMERON MILLS:

Activities: Very active in her church, taught Sunday School at their home; Aunt Kate taught many people in Black River to play the organ.

Burial: Black River Brid, New Brunswick, Canada1

Occupation: School teacher and home maker

Religion: Presbyterian


Notes for JAMES ALEXANDER WILLIAM WATLING:

Ken thinks that Uncle Jim was not well one winter and moved up with Dad in the woods of New Hampshire where he was a "cookee".  His father died in 1915 and he returned from Portland to look after his mother; he had worked in either a chemists shop or a photo shop.  May have been engaged while in Portland.  One story has it that Uncle Jim gave a "diamond" to a girl in Portland prior to his returning home.  Uncle Jim was in the militia anw went to camp in Sussex.


I believe that his father left the home place jointly to sons Jim and Ernest and Jim bought out the other half.    A newspaper clipping reported the following:


`James A Watling of Black River Bridge died Friday, November 25, 1983 at the Hotel Dieu Hospital, Chatham after a lengthy illness.  Born in Black River Bridge, he was the son of the late Jubal and Grace (MacDonald) Watling.


He had been a farmer and, for 42 years, a smelt buyer for W S Loggie Co of Chatham.


He was also the first leader of the 4H club in the area, , a charter member and board member of the former Newcastle Creamery, now Northumberland Co-Op.  He was also a member of St. Pauls Presbyterian Church, Black River Bridge, an elder of the church for 62 years and treasurer of the Presbytery for some time.  His wife, the former Kathryn Mills died in 1965.

He is survived by his daughter, Mrs Alvin Young (Marjorie) of Loggieville; four sons, Winfield, Roy and George all of Moncton, and Neil Watling with whom he resided; 16 grandchildren and by nine great-grandchildren.  The body rested at Adams Funeral Home, Chatham.  The funeral was held at 2 pm Mon. Nov. 28 at St. Pauls Presbyterian Church.  Interment in the Black River Cemetery. 


An undated clipping believed to be from the Moncton Times, reported:

"J. A. Watling

BLACK RIVER BRIDGE (Special) - James A. Watling, 95, died Friday at Hotel Dieu Hospital, Chatham, after a lengthy illness.

Born in Black River bridge, he was the son of the late Jubal and Grace (MacDonald) Watling.  He had been a farmer and, for 42 years was a smelt buyer for W. S. Loggie Co. of Chatham.  He was also the first leader of the 4H Club in the area, a charter member and board member of the former Newcastle creamery, now Northumberland Co-Op.  He was also a member of St. Paul's Presbyterian Church,Black River Bridge, an elder of the church for 62 years and treasurer of the presbytery for a time.  His wife, the former Katherine Mills, died in 1965.

He is survived by a daughter, Mrs. Alvin Young (Marjorie) of Loggieville; four sons, Winfield, Roy and George, all of Moncton, and Neil Watling with whom he resided; 16 grandchildren, and by 9 great-grandchildren.

The body will be at Adams Funeral Home, Chatham, after 7 p.m. today.  The funeral service will be held at 2 p.m. Monday at St. Pauls Presbyterian Church.  Interment will be in the Black River Cemetery."


More About JAMES ALEXANDER WILLIAM WATLING:

Activities: Very active in his church, served as ann elder for over 70 years both in Portland and Black river.  He and Aunt Kate both taught Sunday School at their home;

Burial: Black River Cemetery

Nationality: Canadian

Occupation: As a young man, worked in a pharmacy in Portland; also as a photographer's assistant; returned to Little Branch when his father died (lookked after mother annd Uncle Jim MacDonald) and farmed for the remainder of his life.

Religion: Presbyterian

Notes for JOHN ARCHIBALD MACNAUGHTON:

Veteran of WW1; Major with the North Shore (New Brunswick) Regiment, RCIC; killed in Normandy landing on D-Day; a clipping, source and date unknown, reports: "Major Archie MacNaughton  Word came on Monday to Mrs. J. Archie macNaughton, Black river Bridge, that her husband, Major MacNaughton, had been officially reported killed in action.  No details of his whereabouts were given.

Major macNaughton was an efficient officer and very popular with his men.  He served in the Fiirst Great War as a private with the 104th Battalion.  During peacetime he followed the occuation of farmer but never lost contact with the militia, attennding Summer camp each year.  He went overseas in July 1941.

Major MacNaughton is survived by his wife, the former Grace macNaughton, Black river Bridge; one son, Francis, 14, and a daughter, Margaret, 10; two sisters, Mrs 

Charles Neilson and Miss Catherine MacNaughton both of Chatham Head; his stepmother and one half-sister, Miss Helen MacNaughton, Black river Bridge."

> 

> Monday, December 27, 2004 Back The Halifax Herald Limited

> 

> MacNAUGHTON, Mable Elspeth

> MacNAUGHTON, Mable Elspeth - 74, passed away suddenly in Yarmouth Regional 

> Hospital, December 23, 2004. Born in Dalhousie, N.B., she was a daughter of 

> the late Gordon and Marion MacNaughton. A graduate of Concordia University, 

> Montreal, she was until her retirement, employed with the Public Service of 

> Canada in Ottawa, Ont. She was an active member of the Y'ARC, the Yarmouth 

> Arts Society, and a strong supporter of Women's Centres in both Yarmouth and 

> Vancouver. She is survived by brother, Sidney (Doris) MacNaughton, Yarmouth; 

> sister, Bonnie (Tom) Rogers, North Vancouver, B.C. Cremation has taken place 

> with a memorial service to be held Tuesday, December 28, at 2 p.m. in 

> Sweeny's Funeral Chapel, Yarmouth with Rev. Leslie Robinson officiating. 

> Interment in Black River, N.B., at a later date. In lieu of flowers, 

> donations may be made to Yarmouth Branch of the SPCA. On-line condolences

137.  MARY EDNA BESSIE9 WATLING (WILLARD GEORGE8, WILLIAM MAXWELL7, MARY6 MACNAUGHTON, ALEXANDER5, JOHN4, MALCOLM3 MACNAUGHTAN, JOHN2 MACNAUCHTAN, MACNAUGHTON1 FAMILY)1 was born March 7, 1923 in Point Aux Carr, NB ?1.  She married JOHN FRANKLIN GLENDENNING1 January 27, 1944 in Glendenning's, Little Branch, Black River, New Brunswick1, son of ELMER GLENDENNING and JANE WATLING.  He was born December 14, 1919 in Black River , NB ?1, and died June 21, 1986 in Black River, NB1.


Notes for MARY EDNA BESSIE WATLING:

John met Mary when she attended a service at St. Paul's with her cousin, Millie MacDonald.  He took Mary back to Point Aux Carr that night.  John and Mary were married at the Branch on January 27, 1944 and lived at the Branch for a while.  The day of, or day before, the wedding, John went in town to do some last minute shopping.  It was raining and the roads were icy.  An RCMP vehicle went through a stop sign and clipped John's truck.  


Mary grew up and went to school in Point Aux Carr, she also attended Sunday school which was held in the school.  As part of a large family, Mary took her turn doing all of the chores including those in the barn as well as the house.


Mary was a true partner with john in running their country store; John opened the store about 6:00 am while Mary got breakfast; john went in for breakfast about 8 and Mary took over in the store.  It was a full-time job for both of them.




More About MARY EDNA BESSIE WATLING:

Education: Point Aux Carr, NB

Nationality: Canadian

Occupation: Home maker and store operator

Religion: Presbyterian


Notes for JOHN FRANKLIN GLENDENNING:

John was born at Little Branch shortly after the family returned to Canada.  He attended the Little Branch School probably for 7 or 8 years, and then he worked on the farm.  Dad had not been well for a number of years so Walter and John did the farm work.   


John and Walter hauled fish from Hardwicke to Chatham, cut hardwood on the "ridge" and delivered it to Loggieville and Chatham.  One day they would go to Hardwicke or Baie St. Anne (including Fred Savoie's) and bring fish back to the Branch; the next day, they delivered the fish to Chatham for $5.00 per ton (two box-sled loads).  They went to the woods and cut and split a cord of hardwood and the next day delivered it to Loggieville or Chatham for $5.00 including the cost of the wood.   


John worked hard and put in long hours.  He expected others to do their bit whether it was bringing in the wood for the stove, doing barn chores, going to the woods in winter, making hay in summer, etc.  It was expected and you did it.  But he was also very generous to people needing help.    


In the late 1930's John, along with some other young men from Black River including Tom Harper, Theodore Fowlie, took an agricultural course in Newcastle.  The course included practical farm mechanics, agriculture, book keeping, general mechanics and woodworking.  As part of the course, John made a beautiful birch desk, which he finished in walnut.  I also remember that he learned a new technique for killing chickens.  I believe that it was then or shortly thereafter, that John rigged up a wind generator on the roof of the barn which would charge a battery and provide light.


One winter, November I believe, he and Brydone Weeks (Brydone was home on leave, probably before he went overseas) built a tractor out of an Oakland front end (a car that belonged to Alex MacDonald) and a Godferson rear end (a truck used by the A&R Loggie Co) - and they did this in Walter's old shed without the aid of welding and cutting equipment (or heat). The tractor had two transmissions.  The tractor was very powerful but the too heavy for soft ground.


During the winter of 1945-46, and perhaps winters previous to this, John, Ken and Norm all worked in the woods for Hubert Matchett of Sunny Corner.  They would be gone all week and I did the barn work.


As a young lad going on 17, I went to work on the boats in Chatham loading pulp and pit props.  (I am sure that I got my first job based on the reputation of John, Ken and Norm.)  One day we were storing 8-foot props down in the hold.  They are heavy enough anyway but these were also moldy; I was struggling and John knew it.  John said, "Let me take that one, Donald".  And it happened many times.  


For a number of years John went from farm to farm along the Miramichi pressing hay, first with a horse powered hay press but later with a press operated off a tractor.  (When pressing hay at home, it was my job to keep the horse moving and to prevent it from grabbing at the hay when it went past the end of the bundle.)  Bundles of hay weighed between 80 to 120 pounds.  John worked all around Black River, Napan, Barnaby River, Cains River, etc.  For a number of years, John owned a 3-ton Dodge truck, which I believe he bought from Hubert Matchett, and trucked pulp, pit props, gravel, lime, fertilizer, hay and Christmas trees.



In 1946-47 John and Mary built a new house on the Bob MacLean property and in the spring of 1948 opened a store in their living room.  In 1968 they built a new store nearer the road and put in gas tanks.  John was very entrepreneurial selling groceries, flour, feed, plumbing, furnaces and anything else to make a buck.


John received his call for military service and, I believe, went to Fredericton for a physical.  He had very poor eyesight and was turned down.  He probably would not have had to serve anyway, because farming was an essential service and he was needed at home.  Ken and Norm were already in the Service and I was too young to take over the responsibility of farming.  


When Ken and Norm were in the service, I was the only one around to help with the farm work.  Since Dad died when I was very young, you might say that I learned to work under John's direction.  I recall going to the woods in the winter, sitting on the bob-sled and on the load of wood on the way home, building loads of loose hay and poisoning potato bugs early in the morning to take advantage of heavy dew.  John was the only one of the boys who would sing while driving to and from the fields or woods - I especially remember him singing familiar hymns.


Many of the older people in Black River will remember the 1939 Ford half-ton truck that, as I recall, John purchased from Bonnie Flanagan.  It was a far cry from the old Model A.  The truck had wooden cab on the back and John would take people to town on Saturday night when the stores were open.  There could be a 10-12 people in the back.  I think that he charged one dollar.  The last thing before leaving town, he would drive up Water Street and past Ben's Canteen to see if anyone needed a lift home.  


John and Mary liked to visit family and friends around the Miramichi and Bathurst.  John and Mary, with Margaret and Jeannie, made their first trip to Florida over Christmas of 1958.  Carol and I went with them to help with the driving.  Later, they managed to visit Florida for a month most winters always stopping to see family and friend in Florida and on the way.  When our children were small and we visited Black River, John always had gave the kids a candy or some change just as we were about to drive away.


I did not find an obituary, however, The Moncton Times of  Monday, June 23, 1986 carried the following: 

 "Black River Bridge couple beaten

Man dead, wife hurt in robbery

BLACK RIVER BRIDGE (Special)  An elderly  Black River Bridge man is dead and his wife is in serious but stable condition following what police suspect was a robbery attempt at the couple's home late Saturday.  John F. Glendenning, a local grocer, was pronounced dead on arrival at hospital, while his wife, Mary, remains in stable condition  at Chatham's Hotelo Dieu Hospital.

Both are believed to have been victims of a beating by "several" persons.

Police are saying little about the attack, but confirm several persons were questioned late Sunday before  being released. Robbery is presumed to be the motive for the attack at this point of the investigation.

Mary Glendenning, 62, was apparently tied up by her assailants but managed to free herself to  telephone for help.  No one else was in the house at the time.

The attack occurred sometime between 10 pm.and midnight Saturday.  The Glendenning's store adjacent to the house wasn't broken into, according to one family member. 

Newcastle RCMP are being assisted by General Investigation Section officers from Moncton, RCMP subdivision.  Black River Bridge is about 10 miles from Chatham.

Born in Black River Bridge, Glendenning was the son of the late Elmer and Jane E. (Watling) Glendenning  He was a grocer and farmer, a member of St. Paul's Presbyterian Church, Black River and an active member of the Black River Community Hall.

Besides his wife, the former Mary E. Watling of Point au Carr, he is survived by two daughters, Mrs. Aubrey Gibson (Margaret) of Black River Bridge, Mrs Ray Williston, (Jeannie)  of Moncton; three sisters, Mrs Roy Smith (Grace) of Boston, Mass., Mrs Brydone Weeks (Lillian) of Harvey Station, Mrs Cameron Russell, (Venetia) of Point au Carr; four brothers, Walter of Moncton, Kenneth of Centre Napan, Norman of Black River Bridge, Dr. Donald of Charlottetown, P.E.I.; four grndchildren; several nieces and nephews.

The body will be at Adams Funeral Home, Chatham after 7 p.m. Tuesday.  Funeral service will be at St. Paul's Presbyterian Church at a day and time to be announced. Burial will be in Black River Cemetery".

142.  DOROTHY JEAN9 WATLING (WILLARD GEORGE8, WILLIAM MAXWELL7, MARY6 MACNAUGHTON, ALEXANDER5, JOHN4, MALCOLM3 MACNAUGHTAN, JOHN2 MACNAUCHTAN, MACNAUGHTON1 FAMILY)1 was born October 31, 1930 in Point Aux Carr, NB1.  She married NORMAN PETERS GLENDENNING1 September 27, 1951 in Calvin Pres. Ch., Chatham, N. B.1, son of ELMER GLENDENNING and JANE WATLING.  He was born December 31, 1925 in Black River Br., N. B.1.


Notes for DOROTHY JEAN WATLING:

Jean was born in Point Aux Carr and attended the Point Aux Carr school.  As a member of a large family, Jean did her share of house and farm work.


Norman and Jean are active in their community and are members of St. Paul's Presbyterian Church; Jean has been a member of the woman's Missionary society for more than 40 years and Norman is a Trustee.


At age 59, she got her first drivers license and often drives her sister, Mary, to Point Aux Carr for a few days in Mary's cottage - especially when other family members are home.  Their home and their camp are the scene of family gatherings especially on holiday weekends



More About DOROTHY JEAN WATLING:

Activities: Church, 

Education: Point Aux Carr, NB

Nationality: Canadian

Occupation: Home maker

Religion: Presbyterian


Notes for NORMAN PETERS GLENDENNING:

Norm was born on New Year's Eve, 1925, however, when the birth was registered; it was given as December 31, 1926.  Grandmother Glendenning was a Peters hence his second name.   According to Lillian, Mum and Dad had difficulty deciding between the names Norman and Robert. 


Norman attended the Little Branch School, classes in the Anglican Church in Bay du Vin and the Miramichi Rural High School.  Before school bus service was established, Norm used to take a horse to Bay du Vin, and later Bayside, picking up other students along the way.  The horse was stabled for the day in either Goymer Williston's or Harrison Smith's barn.  


At age 18, he enlisted in the army and found himself in the RCEME being sent first to Camp Utopia outside Saint John, New Brunswick and later to Woodstock, Ontario and Longueil, Quebec.


Norm was a good skater and a good dancer, and usually took me along when I first started going to dances in 1946. I also remember working with Norm at the hay; in the woods on cold and snowy Saturdays; butchering and peddling meat; shearing and dipping sheep and whatever else needed to be done.  


Among Norm's stories is one about Wallace MacDonald throwing a peavey at him and Edwin Adams, trips on icy roads up river with Mr. Harper to tend to a sick horse and the first time he drove a vehicle with airbrakes.


Norman married Jean Watling from Point Aux Carr.  Alton and Eric now operate the farm although Norm continues to "supervise" and run errands; most every day he makes a trip to Miramichi City.  Branchview, the Glendenning farm, is the largest farm east of Miramichi and one of the largest in the Miramichi area.  Branchview has been the recipient of a number of awards.  


Norman and Jean always welcome family to the old family home providing a place at the table for children, siblings, grandchildren, nephews and nieces alike.  He always tells visiting children where the kittens were and offers to give them one to take home.  Many young people of the community earned some money and learned important work habits while working on the farm with Norman and the "boys".  


Norm's early years were filled with long evenings of farm chores on top of full days driving truck for Alfred MacDonald or with John pressing hay.   


  For a number of years Norman served as a trustee of the Black River Community Hall including during a major renovation in the 1960's and reconstruction in the 1970''s; was a director of Agricultural Society #103 and was a Director of the Northumberland Cooperative Dairy.  He participates in local political meetings.  Although not one to travel, in "retirement" he takes time to visit neighbours and friends around Black River.



More About NORMAN PETERS GLENDENNING:

Activities: Church, Community Hall, Northumberland Cooperative, family gatherings

Baptism: June 14, 1926, St. Pauls Presbyterian Church, Black River ?

Education: Little Branch, Miramichi Rural Hign,

Met spouse: Abt. 1950, Dance at Mel Adams, Bayside

Military service: Bet. 1944 - 1945, #          , RCEME, Utopia, Woodstock, Ont, and

Nationality: Canadian

Occupation: Aft. 1945, Dairy Farmer, truck driver.

Children of DOROTHY WATLING and NORMAN GLENDENNING are:

From grannddaughter Tina Newton:


"Newport, NH: George Elbridge Watling, 93, passed away at the Woodlawn Nursing Home, Wednesday, December 13, 2006

George was born to J. Geoerge and Emma (Ronan) Watling on November 24, 1913 in Augusta, GA. In 1919 he moved with his family to this area and grew up in Grantham and Newport attending school and graduating from Newport.  He was employed at the Gordon Woolen Mill for 10 years and at the Dorr Woolen Mill for 25 years prior to his retirement in 1984.  He was a WWII Naval Veteran and a long standing member of the Odd Fellows, the American Legion and the South Congregational Church.

Survivors include two daughters: Christine Newton of Colorado Springs, CO, and Sarah Jane Gunzinger of Perkinsville, VT, 2 grandchildren and several nieces and nephews.  George was predeceased by his firs wife Laila (Anderson) who passed away in 1988 and his second wife Helen (Neimi) Hokkanen whom he married in 1994.  Helen passed away in October of 2004.  A graveside service will take place in the spring.  Thhere will be no calling hours or service at this time.  Memorial donations may be made to the Newport Senior Cemter."
